STATISTICAL APPENDIX

Definitions and Explanatory Notes

The following general notes supplement the footnotes given below the individual tables:

1. In an attempt to bring the material up-to-date provisional figures are included in some tables.

2. Figures in some tables have been rounded off to the nearest final digit. Hence there may be a slight discrepancy between the total as shown and the sum of its components.

3. Differences as compared with previously published figures are due to subsequent revisions.

4. Values indicated within parenthesis are negative values.

5. The following symbols have been used throughout:-

n.a. = not available

— = nil

= negligible

WEIGHTS AND MEASURES

Conversion Factors

British to Metric Units

1 acre = 0.405 hectares (ha)

1 pound (lb) = 0.454 kilogrammes (kg.)

1 long ton (2240 lbs) = 1.016 metric tons (Mt. Ton)

1 hundred weight (cwt) 50.802 kg.

1 mile =' 1.609 kilometres (km.)

1 long ton mile 1.635 Mt. Ton km.

1 lb/acre 1.121 kg./ha

1 cwt/acre 125.536 kg./ha

1 pint = 0.75 litres

1 imperial gallon 4.55 litres

Metric to British Units

1 hectare = 2.471 acres

I kilogramme 2.205 lbs

1 Mt. Ton (1,000 kg.) 0.984 long ton

1 metre 3.281 feet

1 kilometre 0.621 mile

1 Mt. Ton kilometre = 0.612 long ton mile

1 litre = 1.76 pints = 0.219 imp. gallons

1 kg./ha 0.892 lb/acre

Paddy/Rice Conversions

1 bushel of paddy (46 lbs) 20.87 kg.

1 Mt. Ton paddy = 47.92 bushels paddy 0.7 Mt. Ton rice

1 Mt. Ton rice = 68.46 bushels paddy = 1.43 Mt. Ton paddy

1 bushel paddy/acre = 51.55 kg. paddy/ha

(a) The Index numbers are calculated on fixed weights based on the numbers employed as at December 31st,1978. The wage rates used in the calculation of Index Numbers Sources : Labour Department

are minimum wages for different trades fixed by the Wages Boards. Central Bank of Sri Lanka (b) The Index for Central Government employees has been revised based on the number of employees and total salaries as at 1st January, 1986. The Index Numbers from January, 1986 have been spliced. The wage rates used in the calculation ot Index Numbers are the initial salaries and wages in each respective scale.

(c) The Index refers to wage rates of tea growing and manufacturing, rubber growing and manufacturing, coconut cocoa, cardamoms, and pepper growing trades only. (d) Including baking, brick and tile manufacturing, coconut manufacturing, printing, textile, tyre and tube manufacturing, coir mattresses and bristle export, hosiery manufacturing, engineering, garment manufacturing, match manufacturing, biscuit and confectionary, tea export and rubber export trades only. (e) This includes cinema, motor transport and nursing home trades only.

(f) Combined index for workers in agriculture, workers in industry S commerce and services.

(g) A combined index for Non-Executive Officers and Minor Employees.

(h) NWRI = Nominal Wage Rate Index HWRI = Real Wage Rate Index

(i) Provisional

(b) Provisional

Exports are recorded on f.o.b. valuation; imports on c.i.f. valuation. Passage collections by foreign shipping agents and air lines are included in Item 4.1 - Passenger lares. Indicates outright grants received in the form of food aid, commodity aid and project aid. Under Other Private Short- term, credit entries refer mainly to an increase in liabilities resulting from imports under trade credits. The debit entries in respect of such imports are in the merchandise account and are recorded

on an arrivals basis. Debit entries refer to a decline in such liabilities and are recorded at the time of settlement of payment for such imports. The change in capital of the monetary sector is based on local records. The figures shown in the credit column relate to increase in liabilities to the I.M.F. arising from drawings from the I.M.F. Sri Lanka Rupee values reflect changes in the representative rate of the SDR. Figures in the debit column are in respect of repurchases or repayments, made in instalments to the I.M.F., in respect ol earlier drawings. Subscriptions to the I.M.F., consequent to successive increases in Sri Lanka's quota are also shown in the debit column.

(b) Figures upto 1959 include War Loan re-lent to U.K. Government, less the part held by the Currency Board, Central Bank. Figures upto 1952 agree with previously published statistics. Any difference thereafter is due to the inclusion of balances due to Sri Lanka under payments agreement with the People's Republic of China. Except for these balances which are recorded according to local records, the basis of valuation throughout is at face value according to records abroad. Figures since 1985 have been revised to include DST's special revolving credit balances.

(c) As previously published i.e. at face value according to records abroad. Includes foreign assets held by the Sterling Loans Sinking Fund up to 1974. (d) The assets of the Currency Board were transferred to the Central Bank on August 28,1950. Figures upto 1949 are as previously published. Thereafter the figures are as Dublished under the International Reserve in the balance sheet of the Central Bank. The International Reserve also includes balances due to Sri Lanka on bilateral clearing accounts other than with the People's Republic of China and include statutory reserve requirements of commercial banks since January 1993. (e) All figures are according to local records, and upto 1947 are as previously published. From 1949 they agree with foreign balances of commercial banks. The balances are the sum of foreign currency on hand and balances due from banks abroad and export bills purchased and discounted.

Source: Central Bank of Sri Lanka which are not reflected in the balance sheet of the Central Bank (This increases the net external assets of the Central Bank by Rs. 7,062 million at end December 1996).

(ii) to include the balance in the Crown Agent's Account ol the Government (Rs. 2 million at end December 1996). (b) External assets (net) of the Central Bank and commercial banks (including outward bills). (c) Credit extended by the banking system to the Government, net of Government deposits with banks and Government cash deposits.

(d) Currency and demand deposits of the public.

(e) Time and savings deposits ot the private sector held with commercial banks.

(a) This new monetary survey is based on the aggregated data pertaining to both Domestic Banking Units (DBUs) and Foreign Currency Banking Units (FCBUs) of banks operating in Sri Lanka. All data are provisional. Definitional changes have been adopted in aggregating DBU and FCBUs data to avoid double counting and misclassification of assets and liabilities. The major changes are:

(1) All DBU placements in FCBUs are recorded as domestic assets, while all DBU borrowings from FCBUs are recorded as domestic liabilities. (2) Foreign currency deposits of DBUs are classified on the basis of ownership rather than on the basis of currency. Hence, the following apply: (i) One half ( 50 per cent) of NRFC deposits are treated as domestic deposit liabilities; (ii) All RNNFC balances are treated as foreign liabilities;

(a) The required reserves are computed weekly every Friday on the basis of deposit liabilities as on the previous Wednesday. Figures so computed are applicable until next Thursday. Thus, the Required Reserves recorded above refer to the average daily reserves applicable for the last week of the period. The Actual Reserves refer to the reserves that were maintained on the last working day of the period.

(b) Excludes inter-bank deposits. (c) Under Sections 10c, 93. 94, 96 and 97 of the Monetary Law Act (Chapter 422) Commercial banks are required to maintain reserves with the Central Bank and the ratios so maintained shall not be less than 5 per centum or more than 20 per centum in the case of tune and savings deposits and shall not be less than 10 per centum or more than 40 per centum in the case of demand deposits and unused balances of overdrafts allowed. (d) Wiih eftect from 9th August 1985. the reserves required to be held by the Commercial banks in respect of time and savings deposits were to take the form of rupee deposits in the Central Bank provided that an amount not exceeding one eighth of such reserves could take the form of bonds issued by the Development Finance Corporation of Ceylon (DFCC) and held by such banks as an asset. With effect from 26ih February 1988 the reserves required to be held by the Commercial banks were to take the form of rupee deposits in the Central Bank provided that an amount not exceeding one-tenth of its required reserves in respect of the total of time and savings deposits could take the form of bonds issued by trie DFCC and held by such banks as assets.

With effect from 16th September 19B8 the amount of reserves that couiu be maintained in the form of Treasury Bills was increased to 5 per cent of the total deposits. With effect from 11th January 1991 this facility to hold Treasury Bills as reserves was withdrawn.

With effect from 24th January 1992. an amount of till cash over and above two per centum of the total of deposits liabilities but not exceeding four per centum could be maintained as a part of required reserves in the from of Sri Lanka currency notes and coins.

(e) Total till cash less the amount of till cash kept as reserves.

(f) Deposits liabilities have been defined to include foreign currency deposits as well as rupee deposits for computation of reserves, w.e.f. 4th September 1992.

(a) Yield rates prevailing at the end of the period. Source: Central Bank of Sri Lanka (b) This is the rate at which the Central Bank grants advances to commercial banks for their temporary liquidity purposes. After August 1977, each bank, depending on its holdings of selected asset items, was allocated a certain quota of the total borrowings permitted at the Bank Rate. Borrowings in excess of this quota were subjected to a penal rate of interest of 15 per cent per annum. In September 1979, a graduated scale of penal rates ranging between 15 per cent and 25 per cent was introduced. It was further revised upward to a range of 20 to 30 per cent, with effect from April 1980. With effect from August 1980, the Bank Rate was raised to 14 per cent, while the penal rate was raised to a range of 21 to 30 per cent. In March 1983, the Bank Rate was brought down to 13 per cent and the penal rate was fixed at 20 per cent. With effect from 23 November 1983, the practice of granting advances to commercial banks under a penal rate was discontinued. With effect from 30 December 1965, the Central Bank reduced the lending rate on its advances to 11 per cent. A further reduction, to 10 per cent, was made with effect from 07 August 1987. The Central Bank increased the lending rate on advances to 14 per cent with effect from 15 June 1989. The rate was increased again to 15 per cent, with effect from 06 July 1990 and to 17 per cent with effect from 04 January 1991.
